<div id="review_526148618" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/526148618" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Mervosh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/761816-jeffrey-mervosh"><img alt="Jeffrey Mervosh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572619694p2/761816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/526148618?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Mervosh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Mervosh" href="/user/show/761816-jeffrey-mervosh">Jeffrey Mervosh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307276681" href="/book/show/11252905-swamplandia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/761816-jeffrey-mervosh?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer526148618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7507019074940512510">Wow. I loved this book. It is certainly not for everyone, as it is deeply dark and tragic and switches between silliness and terror in the unclear haze of magical realism, but it is also evocative, captivating, and full of wonderfully vivid prose ruminating on love and the loss of childhood. One of the best books and strongest characters (Ava) I have encountered in some time.<br/><br/><b>Update:</b> After two weeks to reflect and think about my review I've down-graded it to four stars. This isn't to take away fr</span>
  <span id="freeText7507019074940512510" style="display:none">Wow. I loved this book. It is certainly not for everyone, as it is deeply dark and tragic and switches between silliness and terror in the unclear haze of magical realism, but it is also evocative, captivating, and full of wonderfully vivid prose ruminating on love and the loss of childhood. One of the best books and strongest characters (Ava) I have encountered in some time.<br /><br /><b>Update:</b> After two weeks to reflect and think about my review I've down-graded it to four stars. This isn't to take away from the book's beauty, arresting narration, or depth of character in Ava. I still highly recommend it as one of the more enjoyable and affecting books I have read in the past few years. This revision is merely an acknowledgment that the book is imperfect. Magical Realism aside, the haze of understanding what gripped and motivated the characters outside of Ava and, to a lesser extent, Kiwi is far too murky. And the ending - which I won't ruin here - is rushed and entirely unsatisfactory given the immensity of events that immediately precede it. Ava, whose emotional intelligence is so high through so much of the book, suddenly seems out of tune with reality. The adults, confronted with something defying explanation, never seek one. Perhaps if the book had ended thirty pages earlier it would have been a more complete novel. Instead, after an incredible first three acts, the fourth feels rushed and the fifth is simply disappointing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7507019074940512510" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_228050198" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228050198" />
    <a title="Megan Baxter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter"><img alt="Megan Baxter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310153693p2/5742086.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Baxter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Baxter" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter">Megan Baxter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

It's the story of a family struggling in uncertain economic times, being squeezed out by a corporate theme park, and the various ways Chief Bigtree and his three children, Kiwi, Osceola and Ava try to cope.<br/><br/>Note: The rest of this review has been withdrawn due to the changes in Goodreads policy and enforc</span>
  <span id="freeText5089894499596289073" style="display:none">Swamplandia is the story of a family on the verge of failure, and the various ways that collapse comes to pass after Hilola Bigtree, mother, wife and celebrated alligator wrestler, dies suddenly. <div id="review_202719630" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202719630" />
    <a title="Paula Margulies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2770009-paula-margulies"><img alt="Paula Margulies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442509046p2/2770009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202719630?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paula Margulies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paula Margulies" href="/user/show/2770009-paula-margulies">Paula Margulies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307263995" href="/book/show/8584686-swamplandia">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202719630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer615383299097944199">A lushly written, quirky, and oddly compelling book, although I felt a little cheated after finishing it, mainly because of the loosely-ended plot. The setting is one of the best I've seen in fiction -- a steamy, mucky Florida swampland theme park called Swamplandia! hosted by the Bigtrees, a family of alligator wrestlers. The main character, Ava, is plucky and devoted to her family, which unravels when her mother, whose dives into an alligator-infested pond is the chief attraction at Swamplandi</span>
  <span id="freeText615383299097944199" style="display:none">A lushly written, quirky, and oddly compelling book, although I felt a little cheated after finishing it, mainly because of the loosely-ended plot. The setting is one of the best I've seen in fiction -- a steamy, mucky Florida swampland theme park called Swamplandia! hosted by the Bigtrees, a family of alligator wrestlers. The main character, Ava, is plucky and devoted to her family, which unravels when her mother, whose dives into an alligator-infested pond is the chief attraction at Swamplandia!, dies. An oddly belligerent and evasive father, Captain Bigtree, a geeky-smart and naive brother, Kiwi, who runs away at 17 to work at a mainland theme park, and a sister, Ossie, who has delusions about romances with dead men she conjures on her Ouija Board, complete the cast of characters. I was with the book until Ossie runs away with an imaginary dredge operator (whose overly long history I skimmed through), and Ava leaves to search for her with a stranger who calls himself the Bird Man. I wondered at the sexual implications of a thirteen-year-old girl and a strange man leading her into the swamp -- why did the inevitable result happen deep in the swamp rather than the first night he was alone with her at her house? Where did all the food they took with them come from (the two sisters were starving up until that point)? And why would Ava so easily give up the red-colored alligator she loved enough to take along on the journey? These questions are never answered, and the discovery of Captain Bigtree's work as a mainland casino showman seems a muted distraction to the last minute rescue of both girls. The story ends abruptly with a couple of pages of summary about the girls and their new lives on the mainland, leaving me wondering what it had all really been about. <br /><br />Switches from first-person (Ava) to third-person (Kiwi) narration aside, this book is beautifully written, with a mythic, hauntingly descriptive quality that echoes the dark, other-worldly environment the characters inhabit. But readers should be warned that the plot-line will take them on a bit of a wild (and, ultimately, unsettling) ride. </span>
  <a data-text-id="615383299097944199" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_149865536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149865536" />
    <a title="Janetmanley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4176147-janetmanley"><img alt="Janetmanley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299444580p2/4176147.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149865536?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janetmanley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janetmanley" href="/user/show/4176147-janetmanley">Janetmanley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307263995" href="/book/show/8584686-swamplandia">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149865536" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2257194719323651012">Although Karen Russell claims her indebtedness to several authors in the acknowledgments of “Swamplandia,” the book was so rattlingly new to me, I didn't see the borrowings. I had a swell time wading through the pseudo-comedy-fantasy, which she centered around an alligator-wrestling theme park in mangrove swamp off Florida's coast, and populated by the eccentric "Bigtree" family, headed up by the indefatigable Chief Bigtree (until he *was* "defatigable"). When I reached a climax of sorts three-q</span>
  <span id="freeText2257194719323651012" style="display:none">Although Karen Russell claims her indebtedness to several authors in the acknowledgments of “Swamplandia,” the book was so rattlingly new to me, I didn't see the borrowings. I had a swell time wading through the pseudo-comedy-fantasy, which she centered around an alligator-wrestling theme park in mangrove swamp off Florida's coast, and populated by the eccentric "Bigtree" family, headed up by the indefatigable Chief Bigtree (until he *was* "defatigable"). When I reached a climax of sorts three-quarters of the way through, I realized how perfectly Russell had set up the book to close in around you. The title, replete with exclamation mark, the sight gags – Kiwi sitting in the mouth of the giant whale on break, Hell’s swimming pools, the Bigtree Family Museum etc. – set up a sort of false enthusiasm for the world that follows. Similarly, the facade of sad, cheap, remote amusement parks that constitutes 11-year-old Ava's world - the world that her parents created for her, far from the "mainland" - disintegrates around her on the furthest point from home of her journey. The overriding theme seemed to be loss of the safety, protection and love that a parent provided – and as far as dead parents go, Hilola BigTree is a hell of a character. More broadly, it captures that interface between childhood and adulthood, in a similar fashion to the Dave Eggers film version of Where the Wild Things Are - I love when authors do that. Quirky but dignified, I am blown away by Russell’s writing, and dismayed at how young she is to have created something so unique and beautiful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2257194719323651012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7669845848818414643">Well . . . well, okay. Okay, no. I mean, yes--she has a seriously goddamn dazzling instinct when it comes to turning an exquisite phrase. And the plot is inventive. And it's set in Florida, land of my sticky swampy childhood (o heart, o little busted swampheart). But . . . she has NO instinct for measuring out her exquisite phrases in palatable dollops; she just smears them all over every page with the mindless wonky-eyed mania of a two-year old frosting a cake--globs and wads of sugarpaste ever</span>
  <span id="freeText7669845848818414643" style="display:none">Well . . . well, okay. Okay, no. I mean, yes--she has a seriously goddamn dazzling instinct when it comes to turning an exquisite phrase. And the plot is inventive. And it's set in Florida, land of my sticky swampy childhood (o heart, o little busted swampheart). But . . . she has NO instinct for measuring out her exquisite phrases in palatable dollops; she just smears them all over every page with the mindless wonky-eyed mania of a two-year old frosting a cake--globs and wads of sugarpaste everywhere, enough so I felt overloaded--bored, even. I mean, in a book so squelchy-full of lovely, lovely things-to-say, I did not dog-ear a single page, coz they all just ran together in a bad applesauce of Poetic Turns of Phrase.<br />THE THING IS, THAT'S MY SHIT. So I feel like a real bummer bein' all down on it. I looooove a fucken squelchy overload of poetic turns of phrase! I looove sentimentality and self-indulgence and shameless melodrama, just love love love it!<br />Which I think is maybe why I will try her short stories coz here's what I felt like the crux was: That that shit is superfly in small doses. But drawn out over the course of a novel, it fails. You get tired of the word "golden," even, which is like one of the best words EVARR especially when you are talking about Florida. We are so golden down there, so sticky swampy golden. <br />But she wants to cram so much of this butter onto the bones of an Inventive Plot and man. No. Not for me, anyway, for me it did not hold.</span>

<div id="review_149226722" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149226722" />
    <a title="A.J. Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2611049-a-j-howard"><img alt="A.J. Howard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325525381p2/2611049.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149226722?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="A.J. Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="A.J. Howard" href="/user/show/2611049-a-j-howard">A.J. Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307263995" href="/book/show/8584686-swamplandia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2611049-a-j-howard?shelf=bookshelf-fiction">bookshelf-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2611049-a-j-howard?shelf=finished-in-2011">finished-in-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149226722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3330185871839652817"><p><i>Swamplandia!</i> begs George Saunders references. Karen Russell shares Saunders fascination with the peculiar Americana of the tourist trap. The titular attraction here is the island home of the Bigtree Tribe, a family of eratz Indian alligator wrestlers. However, whereas the attractions become characters of Saunders' stories, Russell's characters are themselves the attractions of <i>Swamplandia</i>. Their faces appear on the billboards and promotional material and one of the attractions is a museum devote</p></span>
  <span id="freeText3330185871839652817" style="display:none"><p><i>Swamplandia!</i> begs George Saunders references. Karen Russell shares Saunders fascination with the peculiar Americana of the tourist trap. The titular attraction here is the island home of the Bigtree Tribe, a family of eratz Indian alligator wrestlers. However, whereas the attractions become characters of Saunders' stories, Russell's characters are themselves the attractions of <i>Swamplandia</i>. Their faces appear on the billboards and promotional material and one of the attractions is a museum devoted to the families history that features baby pictures and wedding dresses. So when Hilola Bigtree succumbs to cancer, the clan not only loses its matriarch, but also its chief attraction.</p><br /><p>The narrator of much of the novel is Ava, the youngest of the Bigtree children. Russell does a pretty deft trick with this voice. Ava's narrative hints at magical realism but actually depicts an adolescent's disenchantment with the magical world of childhood and confrontation with the harsh and often terrifying world of the adult. However, once this realization is made, this part of the novel loses much of its force and becomes somewhat monotonous. After a certain point, Russell runs out of things to say but the narrative contineus.</p><br /><p>The other portion of the novel is devoted to Ava's brother, Kiwi, who runs away to the mainland in an effort to save the family but finds himself working at a competing amusement park, the Biblically inspired World of Darkness. I found these sections to be ultimately more interesting than Ava's story. However, Russel's narrative structure is somewhat disjointed. Almost the first hundred pages are told from Ava's first person perspective. However, after Kiwi runs away, the chapters alternate between the continued story of Ava and a third-person narrative of Kiwi's struggles on the mainland. The alternating narratives never really coincided in either temporal location or theme and I found this to really disjointed and somewhat clumsy.</p><br /><p>Although I was unimpressed by her first novel I still think Karen Russell is a writer to keep an eye on. The novel suggests that Russell is a great short-story writer, and at times this novel really betrays its roots as an expanded short story. Maybe Russell is one of those writers who are never able to transform their ideas into a cohesive narrative for a full-length novel. Maybe I'm a sucker for a writer for my home town, but I still believe that this is Russell's <i>A.M.</i> (the Wilco debut) a derivative and somewhat disappointing debut that shows promises of future greatness. In the end, <i>Swamplandia!</i> exhibits the fact that Russell has a lot of creative ideas and interesting things to say. Unfortunately the combination of these elements creates a disjointed, muddled, and monotonous disappointment.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="3330185871839652817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Rachel Feryus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18488628-rachel-feryus"><img alt="Rachel Feryus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602876694p2/18488628.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3178775597?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel Feryus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel Feryus" href="/user/show/18488628-rachel-feryus">Rachel Feryus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3178775597&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3178775597" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11216072334839412781"><i>When you are a kid, you don’t know yet that a secret, like an animal, can evolve. Like an animal, a secret can develop a self-preserving intelligence.</i><br/><br/>This book had so much potential and Karen Russell is a talented writer..things just didn’t come together. It took me three tries to make it through to the end. <br/><br/>We start with this slightly dysfunctional family running their alligator park, coping with the death of the mother who held everything together. This I felt was the strongest part of the nov</span>
  <span id="freeText11216072334839412781" style="display:none"><i>When you are a kid, you don’t know yet that a secret, like an animal, can evolve. Like an animal, a secret can develop a self-preserving intelligence.</i><br /><br />This book had so much potential and Karen Russell is a talented writer..things just didn’t come together. It took me three tries to make it through to the end. <br /><br />We start with this slightly dysfunctional family running their alligator park, coping with the death of the mother who held everything together. This I felt was the strongest part of the novel. The main characters interacting, while getting a healthy dose of background/scenery but also getting to know the alligators and how the park functions. I wanted more about the alligators, I thought that was the most interesting. <br /><br />Things sort of fell apart once Kiwi went off on his own adventure to try to bring more money back to the park (and I disliked all of his sections-mostly due to the awful and frequently offensive dialogue), and the sisters split up and it just wasn’t compelling to me at that point. So much of the book is a journey Ava takes to search for Ossie, and while nothing really happens for a bit it seems like there is a magical realism that plays really well with the idea of an alligator theme park and the beauty of the Everglades. So that’s okay, even though I got bored from basically just hearing endlessly about scenery. Then things roughly jolt back to reality, in a bleak way. <br />At the end I guess we are left with a message of resiliency, but I don’t know if I actually believe it. <br /><br />******trigger warning for rape*****</span>
  <a data-text-id="11216072334839412781" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2110810227" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2110810227" />
    <a title="Rebecca Renner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7298629-rebecca-renner"><img alt="Rebecca Renner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560116108p2/7298629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2110810227?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca Renner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca Renner" href="/user/show/7298629-rebecca-renner">Rebecca Renner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10266884-swamplandia">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110810227" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6599645318579125384">I live-tweeted my reading of part of this book (because I'm hunkered down for Hurricane Irma but thankfully I still have power). For a more extensive version of my thoughts on <i>Swamplandia!</i>, you can read <a href='https://twitter.com/Becky_Renner/status/906892866089373699' rel='nofollow noopener'>the series of tweets.</a><br/><br/>In the end, I thought that <i>Swamplandia!</i> was a novel that really should have been a few strong short stories instead.<br/><br/>There was a lot to like about the book. I especially enjoyed the specificity Russell used when she talked about ecology, flora and fauna, and Florida history. T</span>
  <span id="freeText6599645318579125384" style="display:none">I live-tweeted my reading of part of this book (because I'm hunkered down for Hurricane Irma but thankfully I still have power). For a more extensive version of my thoughts on <i>Swamplandia!</i>, you can read <a href="https://twitter.com/Becky_Renner/status/906892866089373699" rel="nofollow noopener">the series of tweets.</a><br /><br />In the end, I thought that <i>Swamplandia!</i> was a novel that really should have been a few strong short stories instead.<br /><br />There was a lot to like about the book. I especially enjoyed the specificity Russell used when she talked about ecology, flora and fauna, and Florida history. Those are some of my main areas of writing and research, and I'm a real stickler about them. So to impress me on that front is a huge deal.<br /><br />What got in the way of me enjoying the book more was, <a href="https://twitter.com/KarenJamestown/status/906964263935213568" rel="nofollow noopener">as Karen Auvinen put it on twitter</a>, <i>Swamplandia!</i> "gets confused about what kind of book it wants to be." <br /><br />There's a dissociation between the tone and diction of the narration from the setting and the events of the story. It's like the narrator is a talking head floating over all of it. She isn't part of the world. She doesn't seem to belong there. Kiwi's sections are different. I honestly really did like his first section in The Underworld.<br /><br />It's a novel by a short story writer, much too episodic and without a strong enough through line.<br /><br />In the end, <i>Swamplandia!</i> wasn't terrible, but it didn't really come through for me. It was too messy, too indecisive. It was a book with a lot of potential that tried to be too many kinds of books at once. If it had done a few of those things well and nixed the rest, this would have been a really stellar book. Instead, the indecision made it just "okay" for me. 3.5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="6599645318579125384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7414295593777985301">A family drama about a clan of gator-wrestlers in the Florida Everglades doesn't necessarily sound like it would be a fascinating read, but in the hands of Karen Russell, it is. <br/><br/>"Swamplandia!" is a humorous dysfunctional family drama, a ghost story, a coming-of-age-story, and a commentary on the gradual destruction of our ecosystems in the name of "progress". It is both funny and deeply moving, at times heart-warming and other times disturbing, and it is beautifully written. <br/><br/>Russell's prose has </span>
  <span id="freeText7414295593777985301" style="display:none">A family drama about a clan of gator-wrestlers in the Florida Everglades doesn't necessarily sound like it would be a fascinating read, but in the hands of Karen Russell, it is. <br /><br />"Swamplandia!" is a humorous dysfunctional family drama, a ghost story, a coming-of-age-story, and a commentary on the gradual destruction of our ecosystems in the name of "progress". It is both funny and deeply moving, at times heart-warming and other times disturbing, and it is beautifully written. <br /><br />Russell's prose has the pace and ferocity that I can only imagine the Everglades possess, a kind of humid sultriness with pleasant surprises and exotic dangers lurking behind every corner. <br /><br />The Bigtree children---Ossie, Kiwi, and Ava---are the strange products of Chief and Hilola Bigtree, whose family-run amusement park, Swamplandia!, has been a landmark of the Glades for decades. It has, sadly, seen better days. <br /><br />After Hilola's unsuccessful battle with cancer, the Chief has taken to long hiatuses on the mainland. When Kiwi, the only male child of the clan, goes off to college, it leaves Ossie, the oldest, and Ava, the youngest, to maintain the amusement park they call home. On most days, they don't see any customers. Then, one day, Ossie goes off in to the wilderness because she has fallen in love with Louis. Ava doesn't like Louis that much. For one thing, he's been dead for over half a century. Poor Ava is left to fend for herself, but she IS a Bigtree, and Bigtrees are known for their resiliency. <br /><br />This is, apparently, Russell's debut novel. I look forward to reading more by her. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7414295593777985301" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_146535739" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/146535739" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146535739?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307263995" href="/book/show/8584686-swamplandia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=orange-prize">orange-prize</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2011">read2011</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146535739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3647060966296854884">When I found out that Karen Russell turned one of her short stories from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47085.St__Lucy_s_Home_for_Girls_Raised_by_Wolves_Stories' title='St. Lucy's Home for Girls Raised by Wolves Stories by Karen Russell' rel='nofollow noopener'>St. Lucy's Home for Girls Raised by Wolves: Stories</a> into a novel, I knew I had to read it. The story this is expanded from is "Ava Wrestles the Alligator," and as far as I can tell, the story can easily be contained within the time period of the novel. It may happen slightly before.<br/><br/>I'm a little torn on this book. I've read some reviews that shred it, and I understand their points, but I think other strengths of the book red</span>
  <span id="freeText3647060966296854884" style="display:none">When I found out that Karen Russell turned one of her short stories from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47085.St__Lucy_s_Home_for_Girls_Raised_by_Wolves_Stories" title="St. Lucy's Home for Girls Raised by Wolves Stories by Karen Russell" rel="nofollow noopener">St. Lucy's Home for Girls Raised by Wolves: Stories</a> into a novel, I knew I had to read it. The story this is expanded from is "Ava Wrestles the Alligator," and as far as I can tell, the story can easily be contained within the time period of the novel. It may happen slightly before.<br /><br />I'm a little torn on this book. I've read some reviews that shred it, and I understand their points, but I think other strengths of the book redeem it from the dismissal they've given it. <br /><br />Pros:<br />I do love the way Russell describes things, and the swamp is mysterious, unpleasant, and beautiful all at once.<br /><br />The idea of expanding a short story. How many times have I wished for that? That allows the reader to learn more about the Bigtrees. See cons for more on this. :)<br /><br />Library boat. These little tidbits create an amazing world of realistic fantasy for the Bigtree children to live in.<br /><br />The little tidbits of beautiful writing, also found in her stories. I'll throw those in at the end.<br /><br />Cons:<br />There is a shocking event around page 260. I saw it coming while hoping it wouldn't. The book finishes without it being dealt with. I hate that! <br /><br />The entire book, actually, ends too soon. Hard to say why without spoilers.<br /><br />Getting to know the Bigtrees takes some of the beauty of the quirky mysterious peek we get in the short story version. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Instead of a kooky sister who sees ghosts, Ossie is just crazy. Instead of a girl who sees magic in the swamp, we see a young girl get violated and used. Instead of a boy who dreams of escaping the swamp for an ivy league, he is ridiculed and sees that he will never measure up. It ends up depressing instead of mysterious. There was never a sense of realism in the short stories, and its presence in the novel ends up leaving a bitter taste in my mouth. At the same time I'm not sure I can fault the author for this. If it had been more fantastical it would have seemed false. What can she do? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I do think it is worth reading. <br /><br />"Hopes were like these ladies, Mom told us. Hopes were wallflowers. Hopes hugged the perimeter of a dance floor in your brain, tugging at their party lace, all perfume and hems and doomed expectation. They fanned their dance cards, these guests that pressed against the walls of your heart....She touched her IV bag and sighed to us that a wallflower in bloom was very very angry, very scary."<br /><br />"I didn't have a normal kid's ideas of the Lord as an elderly mainland guy on a throne. The God I prayed to I thought of as the mother, the memory of love."<br /><br />"Sweating could feel dangerous when you were alone in the swamp, as if droplet by droplet your body might get whisked into the sun."<br /><br />"When you are a kid, you don't know yet that a secret, like an animal, can evolve. Like an animal, a secret can develop a self-preserving intelligence. Shaglike, mute and thick, a knowledge with a fur: your secret."</span>

<div id="review_164748101" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Karin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1692383-karin"><img alt="Karin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1226063586p2/1692383.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/164748101?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karin" href="/user/show/1692383-karin">Karin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer164748101&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164748101" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17965965280326809978">This young author was successful at writing narrative in the first person, characterized simultaneously by the limited perspective and understanding of a child and insights befitting an adult. Her descriptions of the flora and fauna of a Florida swamp suggest an interest or training in botony/zoology, and she writes persuasively and sensitively about her protagonist's parents - a couple trying, and failing, to live outside of society to realize their Utopian dream on the island known as Swamplan</span>
  <span id="freeText17965965280326809978" style="display:none">This young author was successful at writing narrative in the first person, characterized simultaneously by the limited perspective and understanding of a child and insights befitting an adult. Her descriptions of the flora and fauna of a Florida swamp suggest an interest or training in botony/zoology, and she writes persuasively and sensitively about her protagonist's parents - a couple trying, and failing, to live outside of society to realize their Utopian dream on the island known as Swamplandia! <br /> I had two criticisms of this otherwise enjoyable book: First, I can suspend disbelief, but only for so long and under the right conditions...and when an author resorts to stretching it too far (having a 16-year old learn to fly an airplane, and on his first time out, happen upon not one, but TWO of his lost sisters - for whom he was not looking- then execute a perfect landing to rescue them both in the swamps) in order to wrap things up, I feel betrayed. <br /> Second, going for the happy ending is appealing as an author...but when characters are as deeply flawed as these, it is hard to see how any ending not involving Child Protective Custody is not in order. The authors decision to place the two daughters back in the hands of their negligent father is abhorrent to the point of creating in me feelings for this book best described as guttural revulsion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17965965280326809978" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wsievnHiYKuShbhO2BtPH3t/+h1PekhfgRZQ7M0vaKAHF/KXTFjDahrm3sFAYGAk7zDC60LWdlmj6nLRBf6Y3A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

We learn what became of his high school gang—who thrived and who faltered—and we encounter Lou Kline, Bennie’s catastrophically careless mentor, along with the lovers and children left behind in the wake of Lou’s far-flung sexual conquests and meteoric rise and fall. \n\nA Visit from the Goon Squad is a book about the interplay of time and music, about survival, about the stirrings and transformations set inexorably in motion by even the most passing conjunction of our fates. In a breathtaking array of styles and tones ranging from tragedy to satire to PowerPoint, Egan captures the undertow of self-destruction that we all must either master or succumb to; the basic human hunger for redemption; and the universal tendency to reach for both—and escape the merciless progress of time—in the transporting realms of art and music. By the time she and her lifelong friend Zóra begin to inoculate the children there, she feels age-old superstitions and secrets gathering everywhere around her. Secrets her outwardly cheerful hosts have chosen not to tell her. Secrets involving the strange family digging for something in the surrounding vineyards. Secrets hidden in the landscape itself.\n\nBut Natalia is also confronting a private, hurtful mystery of her own: the inexplicable circumstances surrounding her beloved grandfather’s recent death. After telling her grandmother that he was on his way to meet Natalia, he instead set off for a ramshackle settlement none of their family had ever heard of and died there alone. A famed physician, her grandfather must have known that he was too ill to travel. Why he left home becomes a riddle Natalia is compelled to unravel.\n \nGrief struck and searching for clues to her grandfather’s final state of mind, she turns to the stories he told her when she was a child. On their weeklytrips to the zoo he would read to her from a worn copy of Rudyard Kipling’s The Jungle Book, which he carried with him everywhere; later, he told her stories of his own encounters over many years with “the deathless man,” a vagabond who claimed to be immortal and appeared never to age. But the most extraordinary story of all is the one her grandfather never told her, the one Natalia must discover for herself. One winter during the Second World War, his childhood village was snowbound, cut off even from the encroaching German invaders but haunted by another, fierce presence: a tiger who comes ever closer under cover of darkness. “These stories,” Natalia comes to understand, “run like secret rivers through all the other stories” of her grandfather’s life. She’s not sure what she will find - her cousin’s husband, a handsome Englishman, is a stranger, and Noemí knows little about the region. \n\nNoemí is also an unlikely rescuer: She’s a glamorous debutante, and her chic gowns and perfect red lipstick are more suited for cocktail parties than amateur sleuthing. But she’s also tough and smart, with an indomitable will, and she is not afraid: not of her cousin’s new husband, who is both menacing and alluring; not of his father, the ancient patriarch who seems to be fascinated by Noemí; and not even of the house itself, which begins to invade Noemi’s dreams with visions of blood and doom. \n\nHer only ally in this inhospitable abode is the family’s youngest son. She dreams of having the liberty to explore them in writing, and so must find a way to win herself the time and security to use her mind. She escapes her lot by marrying a rich man, but having gained a husband, a house, high society, and a maid, she finds that her new life of privilege is no less constrained. Not only has she taken up different forms of time-consuming labor—social and erotic—but she is now, however passively, forcing other women to clean up after her. Perhaps another and more drastic solution is necessary?\n\nReminiscent of a lost Victorian classic in miniature, yet taking equal inspiration from such modern authors as Jean Rhys, Octavia Butler, Clarice Lispector, and Jean Genet, Amina Cain\'s Indelicacy is at once a ghost story without a ghost, a fable without a moral, and a down-to-earth investigation of the barriers faced by women in both life and literature. It is a novel about seeing, class, desire, anxiety, pleasure, friendship, and the battle to find one’s true calling.\n\n&quot;Indelicacy isn\'t merely a book, it\'s a world; a world I wanted to live in, forever . . . Arch, yet warm; aspiring and impervious; confiding and enigmatic; reposing and intrepid; Cain has conjured a protagonist who purged my mind and filled my heart.&quot; —Claire-Louise Bennett, author of       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/26417.Karen_Russell">About Karen Russell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/26417.Karen_Russell"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1295563225p3/26417.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/26417.Karen_Russell">Karen Russell
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,301
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8955502639353486696">Karen Russell graduated from Columbia University's MFA program in 2006. Her stories have been featured in <i>The Best American Short Stories</i>, <i>Conjunctions</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Oxford American</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. Her first book of short stories, <i>St. Lucy's Home for Girls Raised by Wolves</i>, was published in September 2006. In November 2009, she was named a National Book Foundation "5 Under 35" honoree. I</span>
  <span id="freeText8955502639353486696" style="display:none">Karen Russell graduated from Columbia University's MFA program in 2006. Her stories have been featured in <i>The Best American Short Stories</i>, <i>Conjunctions</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Oxford American</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. Her first book of short stories, <i>St. Lucy's Home for Girls Raised by Wolves</i>, was published in September 2006. In November 2009, she was named a National Book Foundation "5 Under 35" honoree. In June 2010, she was named a <i>New Yorker</i> "20 Under 40" honoree. Her first novel, <i>Swamplandia!</i>, was published in February 2011.<br /><br />She lives in Washington Heights, New York. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8955502639353486696" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
